Yoko Ono the Movie. ü§¶
It's interesting to watch this video as someone who genuinely loved this movie. I went in knowing very little besides the basic concept and its reputation as a classic of avant-garde cinema. I ended up finding it weirdly hypnotic and really enjoyed the experience overall. I wonder if its reputation as film school torture may have affected the way many film students see it. If you go in with this buildup of older students hyping it up as the most agonising class of the year, I can't imagine that you'd really be open to it. I went in with  no idea of what to expect but I wanted to enjoy it. I allowed myself to be drawn in and it worked incredibly well for me.  I'm not saying anyone has to like this, though. I have nothing against James for hating this movie and I completely accept that most people would hate it - for valid reasons. In fact, I agree with the conclusion that this genre offers up the worst movies ever. (I just don't think this is one of them.)

In my opinion, what this film intended is the absolute deprivation and subjugation that the audience feels, and a sense of shame that they chose it themselves.  In fact, the intention of the film's composition is unintentional. The camera allows only forward movement, eliminating the ventilation of pulling back or looking around. As a result, the audience has no choice but to follow the camera unvoluntarily. In this way, the film denies the audience's self-directed interpretation or appreciation, making them only look at the objects that the camera allows. And as the camera gets closer to the subject, the space felt by the audience becomes smaller and smaller. However, as the noise that was initially spread like the background grows bigger and bigger and takes on a threatening form, the dissonance felt by sight and hearing forces us to choose whether to leave the movie theater or completely resign to see it in order to finally overcome it. And the final waving scene finally declares the complete submission of the audience to the film. No one comes out of the cinema without feeling a sense of liberation or fulfillment. Even going out of the movie theater would not have felt refreshing.

it sounds like the film gave you the exact reaction Michael Snow was trying to get.  Wavelength very deliberately sets itself against the idea of film as traditional entertainment.  for structuralists like Snow, the actual content of the film was less important than the techniques used.  Snow is deliberately trying to be maddening.  By putting us in the room, we automatically expect something to happen - this is due to our ingrained notions of what a film should be, most of which have been shaped by Hollywood.  But what if nothing happens in the room?  Of course, your mind started to wander, probably meandering around the frame, observing the room.  And then the person drops dead.  Again, we expect something to happen.  But nothing does.  The woman makes the phone call, and again nothing happens.  Snow is definitely setting us up here, playing against our preconceived notions of film as entertainment and product.  I view Wavelength much in the way I view a film like Dog Star Man or Empire - they're not entertaining in the traditional sense, and in the case of Empire, not even the kind of film you can watch in a single sitting.  But Wavelength is definitely an important film in its ideas - keeping in mind that this was made in 1967, and experimental filmmakers were really toying with the very nature of film form.  I wouldn't say that you should give Wavelength another shot - it's not the easiest thing to sit through.  But I also wouldn't say that there is no reason to watch it.

Oh man, this takes me back to when I specialised in video game studies at university; I specifically studied the works of Philip Glass and Robert Wilson, the man who made a 5 hour long opera with no intermissions called 'Einstein on the Beach' (produced nine years after James' "worst film ever" candidate) that was designed around hypnotic repetition and monotonous audio-visual sensations to induce strong "interior screen" emotions through an intentionally drawn-out lack of audience stimulation.  Their techniques which I applied to my final year game project  (which I ended up receiving top marks for lol) along with a dose of inspiration from Penn & Teller's Desert Bus, remind me so much of the sensations described in this video and it makes me think that it can't be the worst movie ever either, because the people who made this movie 'Wavelength' knew exactly what they were doing just as I did back in university.  And we both succeeded. üòà

The idea of worst movie implicitly asks "what makes a movie, or, to be more general, any kind of art, the worst?" Personally, the idea of a worst piece of art would be one that goes against the intended purpose of art; it would have to be something that refuses to present a genuine idea or purpose. Wavelength, as irritable as it is, is clearly purposeful, and trying to provoke a stimulation. Meanwhile, something that is unable to present anything genuine or noteworthy is ultimately meaningless and frustrating beyond compare. However, a film of such nature I feel, if it does exist, would have to be accidentally made, because when one makes they pretty much have to make a statement no matter one, even if it's accidental. So in that sense, the idea of there being a worst movie seems virtually impossible, because it would just be too inorganic to be feasibly achieveable. You could probably cite a few examples that could come close, but what I'm trying to say here is that the worst film would be literally nothing.
